<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_considerations">Legal considerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Legal considerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aerial_bombardment_and_international_law" title="Aerial bombardment and international law">Aerial bombardment and international law</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_of_1899_and_1907" title="Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907">Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907</a>, which address the codes of wartime conduct on land and at sea, were adopted before the rise of air power. Despite repeated diplomatic attempts to update <a href="/wiki/International_humanitarian_law" title="International humanitarian law">international humanitarian law</a> to include <a href="/wiki/Aerial_warfare" title="Aerial warfare">aerial warfare</a>, it was not updated before the outbreak of World War II. The absence of specific international humanitarian law did not mean aerial warfare was not covered under the <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws of war">laws of war</a>, but rather that there was no general agreement of how to interpret those laws.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> This means that aerial bombardment of civilian areas in enemy territory by all major belligerents during World War II was not prohibited by <a href="/wiki/Positive_international_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Positive international law">positive</a> or specific <a href="/wiki/Customary_international_law" title="Customary international law">customary</a> international humanitarian law.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many reasons exist for the absence of international law regarding aerial bombing in World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Most nations had refused to ratify such laws or agreements because of the vague or impractical wording in treaties such as the 1923 Hague Rules of Air Warfare. Also, the major powers' possession of newly developed advanced bombers was a great military advantage; they would not accept any negotiated limitations regarding this new weapon. In the absence of specific laws relating to aerial warfare, the belligerents' aerial forces at the start of World War II used the 1907 Hague Conventions — signed and ratified by most major powers — as the customary standard to govern their conduct in warfare, and these conventions were interpreted by both sides to allow the indiscriminate bombing of enemy cities throughout the war.<sup id="cite_ref-HARRIS_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HARRIS-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>General <a href="/wiki/Telford_Taylor" title="Telford Taylor">Telford Taylor</a>, Chief Counsel for <a href="/wiki/War_Crimes" class="mw-redirect" title="War Crimes">War Crimes</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuremberg Trials">Nuremberg Trials</a>, wrote that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>If the first badly bombed cities — <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Warsaw_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Warsaw in World War II">Warsaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam_Blitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotterdam Blitz">Rotterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Retribution_(1941)" title="Operation Retribution (1941)">Belgrade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">London</a> — suffered at the hands of the Germans and not the Allies, nonetheless the ruins of German and Japanese cities were the results not of reprisal but of deliberate policy, and bore witness that aerial bombardment of cities and factories has become a recognized part of <a href="/wiki/Modern_warfare" title="Modern warfare">modern warfare</a> as carried out by all nations.<sup id="cite_ref-HARRIS_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HARRIS-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Article 25 of the 1899 and 1907 Hague Conventions on Land Warfare also did not provide a clear guideline on the extent to which civilians may be spared; the same can be held for naval forces. Consequently, cyclical arguments, such as those advanced by Italian <a href="/wiki/General" class="mw-redirect" title="General">general</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Air power">air power</a> theorist <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Douhet" title="Giulio Douhet">Giulio Douhet</a>, do not appear to violate any of the Convention's provisions.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Due to these reasons, the Allies at the Nuremberg and <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokyo Trials">Tokyo Trials</a> never criminalized aerial bombardment of non-combatant targets and Axis leaders who ordered a similar type of practice were not prosecuted. Chris Jochnick and Roger Normand in their article <i>The Legitimation of Violence 1: A Critical History of the Laws of War</i> explains that: "By leaving out morale bombing and other attacks on civilians unchallenged, the Tribunal conferred legal legitimacy on such practices."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy_at_the_start_of_the_war">Policy at the start of the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Policy at the start of the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before World War II began, the rapid pace of aviation technology created a belief that groups of bombers would be capable of devastating cities. For example, British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a> warned in 1932, "<a href="/wiki/The_bomber_will_always_get_through" title="The bomber will always get through">The bomber will always get through</a>".</p>
<p>When the war began on 1 September 1939 with Germany's invasion of Poland, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, President of the <a href="/wiki/Neutrality_(international_relations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutrality (international relations)">neutral</a> United States, issued an appeal to the major belligerents (Britain, France, Germany, and Poland) to confine their air raids to <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> targets, and "under no circumstances undertake bombardment from the air of civilian populations in unfortified cities"<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The British and French agreed to abide by the request, with the British reply undertaking to "confine bombardment to strictly military objectives upon the understanding that these same <a href="/wiki/Rules_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Rules of war">rules of warfare</a> will be scrupulously observed by all their opponents".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2005Chapter_.22Call_Me_Meier.22.2C_p._105_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2005Chapter_.22Call_Me_Meier.22.2C_p._105-36">[36]</a></sup> Germany also agreed to abide by Roosevelt's request and explained the bombing of Warsaw as within the agreement because it was supposedly a fortified city—Germany did not have a policy of targeting enemy civilians as part of their doctrine prior to World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The British Government's policy was formulated on 31 August 1939: if Germany initiated unrestricted air action, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">RAF</a> "should attack objectives vital to Germany's war effort, and in particular her oil resources". If the <i>Luftwaffe</i> confined attacks to purely military targets, the RAF should "launch an attack on the German fleet at <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmshaven" title="Wilhelmshaven">Wilhelmshaven</a>" and "attack warships at sea when found within range".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The government communicated to their French allies the intention "not to initiate air action which might involve the risk of civilian casualties"<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>While it was acknowledged bombing Germany would cause civilian casualties, the British government renounced deliberate bombing of civilian property, outside combat zones, as a military tactic.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The British changed their policy on 15 May 1940, one day after the <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam_Blitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotterdam Blitz">German bombing of Rotterdam</a>, when the RAF was given permission to attack targets in the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruhr Area">Ruhr Area</a>, including oil plants and other civilian <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">industrial</a> targets which aided the German war effort, such as <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a> that at night were self-illuminating. The first RAF raid on the interior of Germany took place on the night of 15/16 May 1940 while the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a> was still continuing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2005Chapter_.22Call_Me_Meier.22.2C_p._111_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2005Chapter_.22Call_Me_Meier.22.2C_p._111-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_war_in_Europe">Early war in Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early war in Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Poland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">Invasion of Poland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Warsaw_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Warsaw in World War II">Bombing of Warsaw in World War II</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zniszczenia1939_0.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Zniszczenia1939_0.jpg/220px-Zniszczenia1939_0.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Zniszczenia1939_0.jpg/330px-Zniszczenia1939_0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Zniszczenia1939_0.jpg/440px-Zniszczenia1939_0.jpg 2x" data-file-width="970" data-file-height="654" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zniszczenia1939_0.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Wielu%C5%84" title="Bombing of Wieluń">Bombing of Wieluń</a>, the first Polish city destroyed by <i><a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a></i> bombing, on 1 September 1939. In one of the first acts of World War II, German bombers destroyed 75% of all the buildings, including a clearly marked hospital and church, killing approximately 1,200 civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-Grab_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grab-43">[43]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the German invasion of Poland, the <i><a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a></i> engaged in massive air raids against Polish cities,<sup id="cite_ref-moral_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moral-44">[44]</a></sup> bombing civilian infrastructure<sup id="cite_ref-moral_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moral-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> such as hospitals<sup id="cite_ref-Grab_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grab-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moral_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moral-44">[44]</a></sup> and targeting fleeing refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-playground_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-playground-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Notably, the <i>Luftwaffe</i> bombed <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Warsaw_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Warsaw in World War II">Warsaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Wielu%C5%84" title="Bombing of Wieluń">Wieluń</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Frampol" title="Bombing of Frampol">Frampol</a>. It has been claimed that the bombing of Frampol was an experiment as according to Polish historians Pawel Puzio and Ryszard Jasinski it had no targetable industry and no military units were stationed there.</p>
<p>In his book, <i>Eyes on the Sky</i>, <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Schreyer" title="Wolfgang Schreyer">Wolfgang Schreyer</a> wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Frampol was chosen as an experimental object, because test bombers, flying at low speed, weren't endangered by AA fire. Also, the centrally placed town hall was an ideal orientation point for the crews. We watched possibility of orientation after visible signs, and also the size of village, what guaranteed that bombs nevertheless fall down on Frampol. From one side it should make easier the note of probe, from second side it should confirm the efficiency of used bombs.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The directives issued to the <i>Luftwaffe</i> for the Polish Campaign were to prevent the Polish Air Force from influencing the ground battles or attacking German territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Speidel.2C_p._18_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speidel.2C_p._18-50">[50]</a></sup> In addition, it was to support the advance of German ground forces through direct tactical and indirect air support with attacks against Polish mobilisation centres and thus delay an orderly Polish strategic concentration of forces and to deny mobility for Polish reinforcements through the destruction of strategic Polish rail routes.<sup id="cite_ref-Speidel.2C_p._18_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speidel.2C_p._18-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Julien_Bryan_-_Look_-_47218.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Julien_Bryan_-_Look_-_47218.jpg/170px-Julien_Bryan_-_Look_-_47218.jpg" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Julien_Bryan_-_Look_-_47218.jpg/255px-Julien_Bryan_-_Look_-_47218.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Julien_Bryan_-_Look_-_47218.jpg/340px-Julien_Bryan_-_Look_-_47218.jpg 2x" data-file-width="726" data-file-height="900" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Julien_Bryan_-_Look_-_47218.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Polish mothers with their newborn infants in a makeshift maternity ward inside a hospital basement during the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Warsaw_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Warsaw in World War II">Bombing of Warsaw</a> by the German <i>Luftwaffe</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Preparations were made for a concentrated attack (Operation <i>Wasserkante</i>) by all bomber forces against targets in Warsaw.<sup id="cite_ref-Speidel.2C_p._18_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speidel.2C_p._18-50">[50]</a></sup> However, the operation was cancelled, according to Polish professor Tomasz Szarota due to bad weather conditions,<sup id="cite_ref-Szarota_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szarota-51">[51]</a></sup> while German author Boog claims it was possibly due to Roosevelt's plea to avoid civilian casualties; according to Boog the bombing of military and industrial targets within the Warsaw residential area called Praga was prohibited.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (February 2017)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup> Polish reports from the beginning of September note strafing of civilians by German attacks and bombing of cemeteries and marked hospitals (marking of hospitals proved counterproductive as German aircraft began to specifically target them, until hospitals were moved into the open to avoid such targeting), and indiscriminate attacks on fleeing civilians which according to professor <a href="/wiki/Tomasz_Szarota" title="Tomasz Szarota">Tomasz Szarota</a> was a direct violation of the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_(1899_and_1907)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hague Conventions (1899 and 1907)">Hague Convention</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Szarota_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szarota-51">[51]</a></sup> Warsaw was first attacked by German ground forces on 9 September and was put under siege on 13 September. German author Boog claims that with the arrival of German ground forces, the situation of Warsaw changed; under the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_(1899_and_1907)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hague Conventions (1899 and 1907)">Hague Convention</a>, the city could be legitimately attacked as it was a defended city in the front line that refused calls to surrender.<sup id="cite_ref-Boog_2001.2C_p._361_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boog_2001.2C_p._361-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (February 2017)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0763,_Warschau,_Br%C3%A4nde.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0763%2C_Warschau%2C_Br%C3%A4nde.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0763%2C_Warschau%2C_Br%C3%A4nde.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0763%2C_Warschau%2C_Br%C3%A4nde.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0763%2C_Warschau%2C_Br%C3%A4nde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0763%2C_Warschau%2C_Br%C3%A4nde.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0763%2C_Warschau%2C_Br%C3%A4nde.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="512" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0763,_Warschau,_Br%C3%A4nde.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> burning after a German <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Warsaw_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Warsaw in World War II">bombing of the city</a>. The <i>Luftwaffe</i> air campaign resulted in the deaths of an estimated 20,000 – 25,000 civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The bombing of the rail network, crossroads, and troop concentrations played havoc on Polish mobilisation, while attacks upon civilian and military targets in towns and cities disrupted command and control by wrecking the antiquated Polish signal network.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Over a period of a few days, <i>Luftwaffe</i> numerical and technological superiority took its toll on the Polish Air Force. Polish Air Force bases across Poland were also subjected to <i>Luftwaffe</i> bombing from 1 September 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 13 September, following orders of the <i>ObdL</i> to launch an attack on Warsaw's Jewish Quarter, justified as being for unspecified crimes committed against German soldiers but probably in response to a recent defeat by Polish ground troops,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and intended as a terror attack,<sup id="cite_ref-Hooton_1994.2C_p._187_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooton_1994.2C_p._187-59">[59]</a></sup> 183 bomber sorties were flown with 50:50 load of high explosive and incendiary bombs, reportedly set the Jewish Quarter ablaze. On 22 September, <a href="/wiki/Wolfram_von_Richthofen" class="mw-redirect" title="Wolfram von Richthofen">Wolfram von Richthofen</a> messaged, "Urgently request exploitation of last opportunity for large-scale experiment as devastation terror raid ... Every effort will be made to eradicate Warsaw completely". His request was rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-Hooton_1994.2C_p._187_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooton_1994.2C_p._187-59">[59]</a></sup> However, Hitler issued an order to prevent civilians from leaving the city and to continue with the bombing, which he thought would encourage Polish surrender.<sup id="cite_ref-Enc_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enc-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 14 September, the French <a href="/wiki/Air_attach%C3%A9" title="Air attaché">Air attaché</a> in Warsaw reported to Paris, "the German Air Force acted in accordance to the international laws of war [...] and bombed only targets of military nature. Therefore, there is no reason for French <a href="/wiki/Retorsion" title="Retorsion">retorsions</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> That day – the Jewish New Year – the Germans concentrated again on the Warsaw's Jewish population, bombing the Jewish quarter and targeting <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogues</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Enc_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enc-60">[60]</a></sup> According to professor Szarota the report was inaccurate – as its author Armengaud didn't know about the most barbaric bombings like those in Wieluń or Kamieniec, left Poland on 12 September, and was motivated by his personal political goal to avoid French involvement in the war, in addition the report published in 1948 rather than in 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-Szarota_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szarota-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frampol_bombing.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/18/Frampol_bombing.jpg/220px-Frampol_bombing.jpg" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/18/Frampol_bombing.jpg/330px-Frampol_bombing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/18/Frampol_bombing.jpg/440px-Frampol_bombing.jpg 2x" data-file-width="516" data-file-height="287" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frampol_bombing.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Before (left) and after (right) aerial photographs of the German <i>Luftwaffe</i> <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Frampol" title="Bombing of Frampol">Bombing of Frampol</a>. The town was completely destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Three days later, Warsaw was surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a>, and hundreds of thousands of <a href="/wiki/Airborne_leaflet_propaganda" title="Airborne leaflet propaganda">leaflets</a> were dropped on the city, instructing citizens to evacuate the city pending a possible bomber attack.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> On 25 September the <i>Luftwaffe</i> flew 1,150 sorties and dropped 560 tonnes of high explosive and 72 tonnes of incendiaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Enc_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enc-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hooton_1994.2C_p._92_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooton_1994.2C_p._92-64">[64]</a></sup> (Overall, incendiaries made up only three percent of the total tonnage dropped.)<sup id="cite_ref-Boog_2001.2C_p._361_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boog_2001.2C_p._361-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>To conserve the strength of the bomber units for the upcoming Western campaign, the modern <a href="/wiki/He_111" class="mw-redirect" title="He 111">He 111</a> bombers were replaced by <a href="/wiki/Ju_52" class="mw-redirect" title="Ju 52">Ju 52</a> transports using "worse than primitive methods" for the bombing.<sup id="cite_ref-Hooton_1994.2C_p._92_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooton_1994.2C_p._92-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smith.26Creek.2C_2004._pp._63-64_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith.26Creek.2C_2004._pp._63-64-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hooton_1994.2C_p._188_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooton_1994.2C_p._188-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Poeppel-von_Preu.C3.9Fen-von_Hase_2000._p._249_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poeppel-von_Preu.C3.9Fen-von_Hase_2000._p._249-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wgreen_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wgreen-68">[68]</a></sup> Due to prevailing strong winds they achieved poor accuracy, even causing some casualties to besieging German troops.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith.26Creek.2C_2004._pp._63-64_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith.26Creek.2C_2004._pp._63-64-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hooton_1994.2C_p._188_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooton_1994.2C_p._188-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The only Polish raid against a target in Germany was executed by <a href="/wiki/PZL.23_Kara%C5%9B" title="PZL.23 Karaś">PZL.23 Karaś</a> light bombers against a factory in <a href="/wiki/O%C5%82awa" title="Oława">Ohlau</a>. The Polish air force left Poland on 18 September 1939 due to the Soviet attack on 17 September 1939, and imminent capture of the Polish airstrips and aircraft stationed in eastern parts of Poland. There was no exception; even <a href="/wiki/Pursuit_Brigade" title="Pursuit Brigade">Pursuit Brigade</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Organic_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic (military)">organic</a> part of the defences of the Polish capital, <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Lublin" title="Lublin">Lublin</a>, one week into the war.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Western_Front.2C_1939_to_June_1940">The Western Front, 1939 to June 1940</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Western Front, 1939 to June 1940">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 3 September 1939, following the German invasion of Poland, the United Kingdom and France declared war on Germany and the war in the West began. The RAF bombed German warships and light vessels in several harbours on 3 and 4 September.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Eight German <a href="/wiki/Kriegsmarine" title="Kriegsmarine">Kriegsmarine</a> men were killed at <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmshaven" title="Wilhelmshaven">Wilhelmshaven</a> – the war's first casualties to British bombs;<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> attacks on ships at <a href="/wiki/Cuxhaven" title="Cuxhaven">Cuxhaven</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Heligoland" title="Heligoland">Heligoland</a> followed.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The 1939 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Heligoland_Bight_(1939)" title="Battle of the Heligoland Bight (1939)">Battle of the Heligoland Bight</a> showed the vulnerability of bombers to fighter attack.</p>
<p>Germany's first strikes were not carried out until 16 and 17 October 1939, against the British fleet at <a href="/wiki/Rosyth" title="Rosyth">Rosyth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scapa_Flow" title="Scapa Flow">Scapa Flow</a>. Little activity followed.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards_V1_p67_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_V1_p67-74">[74]</a></sup> Meanwhile, attacks by the Royal Air Force dwindled to less than one a month. As the winter set in, both sides engaged in <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> warfare, dropping <a href="/wiki/Airborne_leaflet_propaganda" title="Airborne leaflet propaganda">leaflets</a> on the populations below.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Phoney_War" title="Phoney War">Phoney War</a> continued.</p>
<p>The British government banned attacks on land targets and German warships in port due to the risk of civilian casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> For the Germans, the earliest directive from the Luftwaffe head Hermann Göring permitted restricted attacks upon warships anywhere, as well as upon troop transports at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> However, Hitler's <i>OKW Direktive Nr 2</i> and <i>Luftwaffe Direktive Nr 2</i>, prohibited attacks upon enemy naval forces unless the enemy bombed Germany first, noting, "the guiding principle must be not to provoke the initiation of aerial warfare on the part of Germany."</p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Altmark_Incident" title="Altmark Incident">Altmark Incident</a>, the <i>Luftwaffe</i> launched a strike against the British navy yard at <a href="/wiki/Scapa_Flow" title="Scapa Flow">Scapa Flow</a> on 16 March 1940, leading to the first British civilian death. A British attack followed three days later against the German airbase at <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B6rnum" title="Hörnum">Hörnum</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Sylt" title="Sylt">Sylt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Richards_v1_p68_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_v1_p68-78">[78]</a></sup> hitting a hospital, although there were no casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The Germans retaliated with a naval raid.</p>
<p>German <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_France_during_World_War_II" title="Bombing of France during World War II">bombing of France</a> began on the night of 9/10 May. By 11 May, the French reported bombs dropped on Henin-Lietard, Bruay, Lens, La Fere, Loan, Nancy, Colmar, Pontoise, Lambersart, Lyons, Bouai, Hasebrouck, Doullens and Abbeville with at least 40 civilians killed.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Allied light and medium bombers attempted to delay the German invasion by striking at troop columns and bridges, the British War Cabinet gave permission for limited bombing raids against targets such as roads and railways west of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> River.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards_V1_p114-5_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_V1_p114-5-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Legro-135_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Legro-135-82">[82]</a></sup> The RAF bombed <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6nchengladbach" title="Mönchengladbach">Mönchengladbach</a> on the evening of 11 May 1940, while Bomber Command attempted to hit roads and rail near the Dutch-German border; four people were killed.<sup id="cite_ref-Legro-135_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Legro-135-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hinchliffe.2C_2000._p._43_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinchliffe.2C_2000._p._43-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Rotterdam Blitz</dt>
</dl>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/German_bombing_of_Rotterdam" title="German bombing of Rotterdam">German bombing of Rotterdam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-1114,_Rotterdam,_Luftaufnahme_von_Br%C3%A4nden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-1114%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Luftaufnahme_von_Br%C3%A4nden.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-1114%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Luftaufnahme_von_Br%C3%A4nden.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-1114%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Luftaufnahme_von_Br%C3%A4nden.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-1114%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Luftaufnahme_von_Br%C3%A4nden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-1114%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Luftaufnahme_von_Br%C3%A4nden.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-1114%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Luftaufnahme_von_Br%C3%A4nden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="791" data-file-height="493" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-1114,_Rotterdam,_Luftaufnahme_von_Br%C3%A4nden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rotterdam's burning city centre shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam_Blitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotterdam Blitz">German bombing</a>, on May 14, 1940.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Germans used the threat of bombing Rotterdam to try to get the Dutch to come to terms and surrender. After a second ultimatum had been issued by the Germans, it appeared their effort had failed and on 14 May 1940, <i><a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a></i> bombers were ordered to <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam_Blitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotterdam Blitz">bomb Rotterdam</a> in an effort to force the capitulation of the besieged city.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The controversial bombing targeted the centre of the besieged city, instead of providing direct tactical support for the hard-pressed German 22nd Infantry Division (under Lt. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Hans_Graf_von_Sponeck" title="Hans Graf von Sponeck">von Sponeck</a>, which had airlanded on 10 May) in combat with Dutch forces northwest of the city, and in the eastern part of the city at the Meuse river bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> At the last minute, the Netherlands decided to submit and sent a plenipotentiary and other negotiators across to German lines. There was an attempt to call off the assault, but the bombing mission had already begun.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In legal terms, the attack was performed against a defended part of a city vital for the military objectives and in the front-line, and the bombing respected Article 25 to 27 of the Hague Conventions on Land Warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2005-0003,_Rotterdam,_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2005-0003%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2005-0003%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2005-0003%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2005-0003%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2005-0003%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2005-0003%2C_Rotterdam%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="550" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2005-0003,_Rotterdam,_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a> after the German bombing of the city.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Out of 100 <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_111" title="Heinkel He 111">Heinkel He 111s</a>, 57 dropped their ordnance, a combined 97 tons of bombs. In the resulting fire 1.1 square miles (2.8&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of the city centre were devastated, including 21 churches and 4 hospitals. The strike killed between 800–1,000 civilians, wounded over 1,000, and made 78,000 homeless.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Nearly twenty-five thousand homes, 2,320 stores, 775 warehouses and 62 schools were destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Whilst German historian <a href="/wiki/Horst_Boog" title="Horst Boog">Horst Boog</a> says <a href="/wiki/British_propaganda_during_World_War_II" title="British propaganda during World War II">British propaganda</a> inflated the number of civilian casualties by a factor of 30,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> contemporary newspaper reports show the Dutch legation in Paris initially estimated 100,000 people were killed,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> the Dutch legation in New York later issued a revised figure of 30,000.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> International news agencies widely reported these figures, portraying Rotterdam as a city mercilessly destroyed by terror bombing without regard for civilian life, with 30,000 dead lying under the ruins.<sup id="cite_ref-Hinchliffe.2C_2000._p._43_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinchliffe.2C_2000._p._43-83">[83]</a></sup> Neither claim was true. Furthermore, the bombing was against well-defined targets, albeit in the middle of the city, and would have assisted the advancing German Army.<sup id="cite_ref-Hinchliffe.2C_2000._p._43_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinchliffe.2C_2000._p._43-83">[83]</a></sup> The Germans had threatened to bomb <a href="/wiki/Utrecht_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Utrecht (city)">Utrecht</a> in the same fashion, and the Netherlands surrendered.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Allied_bombing_of_Germany"></span></p>
<dl>
<dt>Allied response</dt>
</dl>
<p>Following the attack on Rotterdam, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">RAF Bomber Command</a> was authorized to attack German targets east of the Rhine on 15 May 1940; the Air Ministry authorized <a href="/wiki/Air_Marshal" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Marshal">Air Marshal</a> Charles Portal to attack targets in the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr" title="Ruhr">Ruhr</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a> plants and other civilian <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">industrial</a> targets which aided the German war effort, such as <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2005Chapter_.22Call_Me_Meier.22.2C_p._111_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2005Chapter_.22Call_Me_Meier.22.2C_p._111-42">[42]</a></sup> The underlying motive for the attacks was to divert German air forces away from the land front.<sup id="cite_ref-Boog_2001.2C_p._362_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boog_2001.2C_p._362-99">[99]</a></sup> Churchill explained the rationale of his decision to his French counterparts in a letter dated the 16th: "I have examined today with the War Cabinet and all the experts the request which you made to me last night and this morning for further fighter squadrons. We are all agreed that it is better to draw the enemy on to this Island by striking at his vitals, and thus to aid the common cause."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Due to the inadequate British bomb-sights the strikes that followed "had the effect of terror raids on towns and villages."<sup id="cite_ref-Boog_2001.2C_p._362_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boog_2001.2C_p._362-99">[99]</a></sup> On the night of 15/16 May, 96 bombers crossed the Rhine and attacked targets in <a href="/wiki/Gelsenkirchen" title="Gelsenkirchen">Gelsenkirchen</a>. 78 had been assigned oil targets, but only 24 claimed to have accomplished their objective.<sup id="cite_ref-Jane.27s.2C_1989._p._34_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jane.27s.2C_1989._p._34-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Richards_V1_p124_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_V1_p124-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> On the night of 17/18 May, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">RAF Bomber Command</a> bombed oil installations in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a>; the H.E. and 400 incendiaries dropped caused six large, one moderately large and 29 small fires. As a result of the attack, 47 people were killed and 127 were wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HA-als_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HA-als-105">[105]</a></sup> Railway yards at Cologne were attacked on the same night.<sup id="cite_ref-HA-als_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HA-als-105">[105]</a></sup> During May, <a href="/wiki/Essen" title="Essen">Essen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duisburg" title="Duisburg">Duisburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hanover" title="Hanover">Hanover</a> were attacked in a similar fashion by Bomber Command. In June, attacks were made on <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bochum" title="Bochum">Bochum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jane.27s.2C_1989._p._34_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jane.27s.2C_1989._p._34-101">[101]</a></sup> At the time, Bomber Command lacked the necessary navigational and bombing technical background and the accuracy of the bombings during the night attacks was abysmal. Consequently, the bombs were usually scattered over a large area, causing an uproar in Germany. On the night of 7/8 June 1940 a single <a href="/wiki/French_Naval_Aviation" title="French Naval Aviation">French Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Farman_F.223" class="mw-redirect" title="Farman F.223">Farman F.223</a> aircraft bombed <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, the first Allied attack on the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The attack was in retaliation for a German bombing of <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> on 3 June.</p>
<p>Despite the British attacks on German cities, the Luftwaffe did not begin to attack military and economic targets in the UK until six weeks after the campaign in France was concluded.<sup id="cite_ref-Boog_2001.2C_p._362_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boog_2001.2C_p._362-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Battle_of_Britain_and_the_Blitz">The Battle of Britain and the Blitz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The Battle of Britain and the Blitz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Air_Raid_Damage_in_Britain_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36220A.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Air_Raid_Damage_in_Britain_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36220A.jpg/220px-Air_Raid_Damage_in_Britain_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36220A.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Air_Raid_Damage_in_Britain_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36220A.jpg/330px-Air_Raid_Damage_in_Britain_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36220A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Air_Raid_Damage_in_Britain_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36220A.jpg/440px-Air_Raid_Damage_in_Britain_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36220A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="632" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Air_Raid_Damage_in_Britain_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36220A.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The undamaged St Paul's Cathedral surrounded by smoke and bombed-out buildings and houses in December 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 22 June 1940, France signed an armistice with Germany. Britain was determined to keep fighting. On 1/2 July, the British attacked the German warships <a href="/wiki/German_battleship_Scharnhorst" title="German battleship Scharnhorst"><i>Scharnhorst</i></a><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/German_cruiser_Prinz_Eugen" title="German cruiser Prinz Eugen"><i>Prinz Eugen</i></a><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> in the port of <a href="/wiki/Kiel" title="Kiel">Kiel</a><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and the next day, 16 RAF bombers attacked German train facilities in <a href="/wiki/Hamm" title="Hamm">Hamm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a> began in early June 1940 with small scale bombing raids on Britain. These <i>Störangriffe</i> ("nuisance raids") were used to train bomber crews in both day and night attacks, to test defences and try out methods. These training flights continued through July and August, and into the first week of September.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201371.E2.80.9372_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201371.E2.80.9372-111">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a>'s general order, issued on 30 June 1940, stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The war against England is to be restricted to destructive attacks against industry and air force targets which have weak defensive forces. ... The most thorough study of the target concerned, that is vital points of the target, is a pre-requisite for success. It is also stressed that every effort should be made to avoid unnecessary loss of life amongst the civilian population.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Hermann Göring<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Kanalkampf" title="Kanalkampf">Kanalkampf</a></i> of attacks on shipping and fighter skirmishes over the English Channel started on 4 July, and escalated on 10 July, a day which Dowding later proposed as the official start date for the Battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200161.E2.80.9362_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200161.E2.80.9362-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Throughout the battle, Hitler called for the British to accept peace, but they refused to negotiate.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Still hoping that the British would negotiate for peace, Hitler explicitly prohibited attacks on London and against civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-Boog_2001.2C_p._362_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boog_2001.2C_p._362-99">[99]</a></sup> Any airmen who, intentionally or unintentionally, violated this order were punished.<sup id="cite_ref-Boog_2001.2C_p._362_99-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boog_2001.2C_p._362-99">[99]</a></sup> Hitler's No. 17 Directive, issued 1 August 1940, established the conduct of war against Britain and specifically forbade the <i>Luftwaffe</i> from conducting terror raids. The <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="Führer">Führer</a> declared that terror attacks could only be a means of reprisal, as ordered by him.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 6 August Göring finalised plans for <a href="/wiki/Adlertag" title="Adlertag">"Operation Eagle Attack"</a> with his commanders: destruction of RAF Fighter Command across the south of England was to take four days, then bombing of military and economic targets was to systematically extend up to the Midlands until daylight attacks could proceed unhindered over the whole of Britain, then a major attack was to be made on London causing a crisis with refugees when the intended <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a> invasion was due to begin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200156.E2.80.9357.2C_61.E2.80.9362_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200156.E2.80.9357.2C_61.E2.80.9362-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201382.E2.80.9385_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201382.E2.80.9385-119">[119]</a></sup> On 8 August 1940, the Germans switched to raids on RAF fighter bases.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> To reduce losses, the <i>Luftwaffe</i> also began to use increasing numbers of bombers at night.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Ray_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Ray-121">[121]</a></sup> From the night of 19/20 August night bombing targeted the aircraft industry, ports, harbours, and other strategic targets in towns and cities, including suburban areas around London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201382.E2.80.9383_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201382.E2.80.9383-122">[122]</a></sup> By the last week of August, over half the missions were flown under the cover of dark. On 24 August, fate took a turn, and several off-course German bombers accidentally bombed central areas of London.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The next day, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">RAF</a> bombed Berlin for the first time, targeting <a href="/wiki/Tempelhof_airfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Tempelhof airfield">Tempelhof airfield</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> factories in Siemenstadt.<sup id="cite_ref-Richard_Overy_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Overy-126">[126]</a></sup> These attacks were seen by the Germans as indiscriminate due to their inaccuracy, and this infuriated Hitler;<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> he ordered that the 'night piracy of the British' be countered by a concentrated night offensive against the island, and especially London.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_2004._p._122_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_2004._p._122-130">[130]</a></sup> In a public speech in Berlin on 4 September 1940, Hitler announced that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The other night the English had bombed Berlin. So be it. But this is a game at which two can play. When the British Air Force drops 2000 or 3000 or 4000 kg of bombs, then we will drop 150 000, 180 000, 230 000, 300 000, 400 000 kg on a single night. When they declare they will attack our cities in great measure, we will eradicate their cities. The hour will come when one of us will break – and it will not be <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_German_Workers_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="National Socialist German Workers Party">National Socialist</a> Germany!</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Adolf Hitler<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg/220px-Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg" width="220" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg/330px-Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg/440px-Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4472" data-file-height="4420" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
German <i>Luftwaffe</i> <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_111" title="Heinkel He 111">Heinkel He 111</a> bomber flying over Wapping and the Isle of Dogs in the East End of <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> at the start of the Luftwaffe's evening raids of 7 September 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">The Blitz</a> was underway.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Göring – at <a href="/wiki/Albert_Kesselring" title="Albert Kesselring">Kesselring</a>'s urging and with Hitler's support- turned to a massive assault on the British capital.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983.2C_p._52_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983.2C_p._52-17">[17]</a></sup> On 7 September 318 bombers from the whole <a href="/wiki/KG_53" class="mw-redirect" title="KG 53">KG 53</a> supported by eight other <i>Kampfgruppen</i>, flew almost continuous sorties against London, the dock area which was already in flames from earlier daylight attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_2004._p._122_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_2004._p._122-130">[130]</a></sup> The attack of 7 September 1940 did not entirely step over the line into a clear terror bombing effort since its primary target was the London docks, but there was clearly an assumed hope of terrorizing the London population.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983.2C_p._52_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983.2C_p._52-17">[17]</a></sup> Hitler himself hoped that the bombing of London would terrorize the population into submission. He stated that "If eight million [Londoners] go mad, it might very well turn into a catastrophe!". After that he believed "even a small invasion might go a long way".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Another 250 bomber sorties were flown in the night. By the morning of 8 September 430 Londoners had been killed. The <i>Luftwaffe</i> issued a press notice announcing they had dropped more than 1,000,000 kilograms of bombs on London in 24 hours. Many other British cities were hit in the nine-month Blitz, including <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Blitz" title="Plymouth Blitz">Plymouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Blitz" title="Birmingham Blitz">Birmingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_Blitz" title="Liverpool Blitz">Liverpool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southampton_Blitz" title="Southampton Blitz">Southampton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Blitz" title="Manchester Blitz">Manchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Blitz" title="Bristol Blitz">Bristol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belfast_Blitz" title="Belfast Blitz">Belfast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_Blitz" title="Cardiff Blitz">Cardiff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clydebank_Blitz" title="Clydebank Blitz">Clydebank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hull_Blitz" title="Hull Blitz">Kingston upon Hull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Blitz" title="Coventry Blitz">Coventry</a>. <a href="/wiki/Basil_Collier" title="Basil Collier">Basil Collier</a>, author of 'The Defence of the United Kingdom', the HMSO's official history, wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Although the plan adopted by the <i>Luftwaffe</i> early September had mentioned attacks on the population of large cities, detailed records of the raids made during the autumn and the winter of 1940–41 does not suggest that indiscriminate bombing of the civilians was intended. The points of aim selected were largely factories and docks. Other objectives specifically allotted to bomber-crews included the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a> and the governmental quarter round <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a>.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Basil_Collier" title="Basil Collier">Basil Collier</a><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In addition to the conclusions of Basil Collier to that effect there are also, for example, the 1949 memoirs of General <a href="/wiki/Henry_H._Arnold" title="Henry H. Arnold">Henry H. Arnold</a> who had been in London in 1941 and supported Collier's estimate. <a href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Harris,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Arthur Harris, 1st Baronet">Harris</a> noted in 1947 that the Germans had failed to take the opportunity to destroy English cities by concentrated incendiary bombing.<sup id="cite_ref-Boog_2001.2C_p._365_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boog_2001.2C_p._365-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the war continued, an escalating <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_beams" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of the beams">war of electronic technology</a> developed. To counter German radio navigation aids, which helped their navigators find targets in the dark and through <a href="/wiki/Overcast" title="Overcast">cloud cover</a>, the British raced to work out the problems with countermeasures (most notably airborne <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>, as well as highly effective deceptive beacons and jammers).<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NA-306-NT-3163V.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/NA-306-NT-3163V.jpg/220px-NA-306-NT-3163V.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/NA-306-NT-3163V.jpg/330px-NA-306-NT-3163V.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/NA-306-NT-3163V.jpg/440px-NA-306-NT-3163V.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1093" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NA-306-NT-3163V.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Children in the east end of London, made homeless by the random bombs of the Nazi night raiders, waiting outside the wreckage of what was their home". September 1940 (National Archives)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite causing a great deal of damage and disrupting the daily lives of the civilian population, the bombing of Britain failed to have an impact. British <a href="/wiki/Air_defense" class="mw-redirect" title="Air defense">air defenses</a> became more formidable, and attacks tapered off as Germany abandoned its efforts against Britain and focused more on the Soviet Union.</p>
<p>Operation Abigail Rachel was the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Mannheim_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Mannheim in World War II">bombing of Mannheim</a> the "first deliberate <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terror raid</a>" on Germany on 16 December.<sup id="cite_ref-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508-137">[137]</a></sup> The British had been waiting for the opportunity to experiment with such a raid aimed at creating a maximum of destruction in a selected town since the summer of 1940, and the opportunity was given after the German raid on Coventry.<sup id="cite_ref-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508-137">[137]</a></sup> Internally it was declared to be a reprisal for Coventry and Southampton.<sup id="cite_ref-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508-137">[137]</a></sup> The new bombing policy was officially ordered by Churchill at the start of December, on condition it receive no publicity and be considered an experiment.<sup id="cite_ref-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508_137-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508-137">[137]</a></sup> Target marking and most bombs missed the city centre.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> This led to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Bomber_stream" title="Bomber stream">bomber stream</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508_137-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508-137">[137]</a></sup> Despite the lack of decisive success of this raid, approval was granted for further Abigails.<sup id="cite_ref-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508_137-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horst_Boog_p_507.2C_508-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>This was the start of a British drift away from precision attacks on military targets and towards area bombing attacks on whole cities.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany_later_in_the_war">Germany later in the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Germany later in the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Steinbock" title="Operation Steinbock">Operation Steinbock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vergeltungswaffe" class="mw-redirect" title="Vergeltungswaffe">Vergeltungswaffen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_177" title="Heinkel He 177">Heinkel He 177</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bomber_B" title="Bomber B">Bomber B</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amerika_Bomber" title="Amerika Bomber">Amerika Bomber</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HE_177_ground.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/HE_177_ground.JPG/220px-HE_177_ground.JPG" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/HE_177_ground.JPG/330px-HE_177_ground.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/HE_177_ground.JPG/440px-HE_177_ground.JPG 2x" data-file-width="975" data-file-height="537" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HE_177_ground.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A captured Heinkel He 177A in French <i>Armee de l'Air</i> colors in 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to the pre-war death of <i>Generalleutnant</i> <a href="/wiki/Walther_Wever_(general)" title="Walther Wever (general)">Walther Wever</a> in 1936, the primary proponent of a strategic bombing capability for Hitler's new air force, virtually nothing serious had been done with building any sort of truly-"four-engined" <a href="/wiki/Heavy_bomber" title="Heavy bomber">heavy bomber</a> as the Allies possessed before the war began. The only "heavy bomber" design that would see service with the <i>Luftwaffe</i> in World War II – the trouble-prone <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_177" title="Heinkel He 177">Heinkel He 177</a>A <i>Greif</i> (griffin) – had also been mistakenly meant by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Aviation_(Nazi_Germany)" title="Ministry of Aviation (Nazi Germany)">RLM</a> to have a medium angle "dive bombing" capability from the design's start in November 1937, something which <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Heinkel" title="Ernst Heinkel">Ernst Heinkel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erhard_Milch" title="Erhard Milch">Erhard Milch</a> had vehemently disagreed with, which would not be rescinded until September 1942 by Goering himself.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The He 177A went into service in April 1942, despite an ongoing series of engine fires in the prototype series of aircraft, and the small batch of A-0 series production prototypes leading up to that timeframe – a serious enough deficiency, along with numerous, seriously deficient design features in the 1.5-tonnes apiece <a href="/wiki/Daimler-Benz_DB_606" class="mw-redirect" title="Daimler-Benz DB 606">Daimler-Benz DB 606</a> engines' nacelle accommodations — to lead <i>Reichsmarschall</i> <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a> to decry the He 177A's pair of DB 606 powerplants as nothing more than fire-prone, cumbersome <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_177#Engine_difficulties" title="Heinkel He 177">"welded-together engines"</a> in August of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:V-2victimAntwerp1944.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/V-2victimAntwerp1944.jpg/220px-V-2victimAntwerp1944.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/V-2victimAntwerp1944.jpg/330px-V-2victimAntwerp1944.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/V-2victimAntwerp1944.jpg/440px-V-2victimAntwerp1944.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2773" data-file-height="2153" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:V-2victimAntwerp1944.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of the victims of a German <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2 rocket</a> that struck Teniers Square, Antwerp, Belgium on 27 November 1944</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As the He 177As entered service in April 1942, following <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_L%C3%BCbeck_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Lübeck in World War II">a destructive RAF attack</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic</a> medieval city of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a>, Adolf Hitler ordered the <i>Luftwaffe</i> to retaliate, leading to the so-called <a href="/wiki/Baedeker_Blitz" title="Baedeker Blitz">Baedeker Blitz</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Price.2C_2005._p._195_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price.2C_2005._p._195-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Führer has ordered that the air war against England be given a more aggressive stamp. Accordingly, when targets are being selected, preference is to be given to those where attacks are likely to have the greatest possible effect on civilian life. Besides raids on ports and industry, terror attacks of retaliatory nature are to be carried out against towns other than London. Minelaying is to be scaled down in favour of these attacks.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Signal from the Führer's headquarters to the <i>Luftwaffe</i> High Command, 14 April 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-Price.2C_2005._p._195_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price.2C_2005._p._195-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:London_V2_Frissell2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/London_V2_Frissell2.jpg/220px-London_V2_Frissell2.jpg" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/London_V2_Frissell2.jpg/330px-London_V2_Frissell2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/London_V2_Frissell2.jpg/440px-London_V2_Frissell2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="983" data-file-height="1030" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:London_V2_Frissell2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aftermath of <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2</a> bombing at <a href="/wiki/Battersea" title="Battersea">Battersea</a>, London, 27 January 1945.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 1944, a beleaguered Germany tried to strike a blow to British morale with terror bombing with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Steinbock" title="Operation Steinbock">Operation Steinbock</a>, nicknamed the "Baby Blitz" by the British. At this stage of the war, Germany was critically short of heavy and medium bombers, with the added obstacles of a highly effective and sophisticated British air-defence system, and the increasing vulnerability of airfields in occupied Western Europe to Allied air attack making the effectiveness of German retaliation more doubtful.</p>
<p>Following not only General Wever's June 1936 death, but also the pre-war cancellation of the <a href="/wiki/Ural_bomber" title="Ural bomber">Ural bomber</a> program that Wever supported to develop a German version of a four-engined strategic bomber airframe, the 1939-begun <i><a href="/wiki/Bomber_B" title="Bomber B">Bomber B</a></i> program sought to produce a twin-engined strategic bomber intended for European combat, that while it could carry nearly-equivalent bombloads to those of the Allied four-engined "heavies", were intended for shorter range sorties, as a general replacement for, and as an advanced development of the pre-war <i><a href="/wiki/Schnellbomber" title="Schnellbomber">Schnellbomber</a></i> concept, with the <i>Bomber B</i> designs meant to achieve top level speeds of at least 600&#160;km/h (370&#160;mph), significantly faster than their <i>Schnellbomber</i> ancestors. The <i>Bomber B</i> program went nowhere, as the intended designs required pairs of combat-reliable aviation engines of at least 1,500&#160;kW (2,000 PS) apiece, something that the German aviation engine industry had <a href="/wiki/Bomber_B#High-output_aviation_engines_for_the_Luftwaffe" title="Bomber B">insurmountable problems in developing</a>. A further design program initiated in the late spring of 1942, intended for the Luftwaffe to have trans-Atlantic ranged four-engined (and later six-engined) bombers to attack the continental United States and aptly named the <i><a href="/wiki/Amerika_Bomber" title="Amerika Bomber">Amerika Bomber</a></i>, also went nowhere, with only five prototype airframes getting airborne for testing from two design competitors, before the war's end. Following the August 1942-dated "welded-together engines" complaints from Goering concerning the He 177A's engine problems delaying its full service introduction, the only comparable German four-engined "heavy bomber" design — meant specifically from the start to be a "four-engine heavy" and not a conversion of any other mission-specific type of aircraft — to even be prototyped into a flyable airframe design, that was in any way closely similar to the frontline Allied types, with four "separate" engines, was the <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_177#Further_development-the_Heinkel_He_177B" title="Heinkel He 177">Heinkel He 177B</a>. These were created in the form of the He 177 V101 through V104 prototypes, all rebuilt from earlier He 177A airframes. Conversion of these airframes were started early in 1943, with the first He 177B prototype to fly, the He 177 V102, making its maiden flight late in December 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Production of the B-series by Heinkel's only subcontractor for the <i>Greif</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Arado_Flugzeugwerke" title="Arado Flugzeugwerke">Arado Flugzeugwerke</a></i>, would not have started until November 1944, because of Arado's focus on the production of its own <a href="/wiki/Arado_Ar_234" title="Arado Ar 234">Arado Ar 234</a> jet-powered reconnaissance-bomber at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The July 1944-initiated <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Fighter_Program" title="Emergency Fighter Program"><i>Jägernotprogramm</i></a>, as well as the devastating effects of Allied bombing on the entire German aviation industry, prevented any production of the He 177B design.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-2020,_Wei%C3%9Frussland,_Minsk,_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-2020%2C_Wei%C3%9Frussland%2C_Minsk%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-2020%2C_Wei%C3%9Frussland%2C_Minsk%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-2020%2C_Wei%C3%9Frussland%2C_Minsk%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-2020%2C_Wei%C3%9Frussland%2C_Minsk%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-2020%2C_Wei%C3%9Frussland%2C_Minsk%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-2020%2C_Wei%C3%9Frussland%2C_Minsk%2C_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="606" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-2020,_Wei%C3%9Frussland,_Minsk,_Zerst%C3%B6rungen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Minsk" title="Minsk">Minsk</a> in 1941 after German bombing. 85% of the city was completely destroyed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, German scientists had invented <a href="/wiki/Vergeltungswaffe" class="mw-redirect" title="Vergeltungswaffe">vengeance weapons</a> – <a href="/wiki/V-1_flying_bomb" title="V-1 flying bomb">V-1 flying bombs</a> and <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2 ballistic missiles</a> – and these were used to launch an aerial assault on London and other cities in southern England from continental Europe. The campaign was much less destructive than the Blitz, so the British called it 'the Baby Blitz'. As the Allies advanced across France and towards Germany from the West, Paris, <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge" title="Liège">Liège</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lille" title="Lille">Lille</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> also became targets.</p>
<p>The British and US directed part of the strategic bombing to the eradication of "wonder weapon" threats in what was later known as <a href="/wiki/Operation_Crossbow" title="Operation Crossbow">Operation Crossbow</a>. The development of the V2 was hit preemptively in the British <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Peenem%C3%BCnde_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Peenemünde in World War II">Peenemünde Raid (Operation Hydra)</a> of August 1943.</p>
<p>British historian <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Taylor_(historian)" title="Frederick Taylor (historian)">Frederick Taylor</a> asserts that "all sides bombed each other's cities during the war. Half a million <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> citizens, for example, died from German bombing during the invasion and occupation of Russia. That's roughly equivalent to the number of German citizens who died from Allied raids".<sup id="cite_ref-Hawley_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawley-8">[8]</a></sup> The Luftwaffe destroyed numerous Soviet cities through bombing, including Minsk, Sevastopol and Stalingrad. 20,528 tons of bombs were dropped on Sevastopol in June 1942 alone.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> German bombing efforts on the Eastern Front dwarfed its commitments in the west. For example, the Luftwaffe dropped over 100,000 tons of bombs upon the Soviet Union from 25 June 1941 to the end of November 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooton201668_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooton201668-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_British_later_in_the_war">The British later in the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The British later in the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
