<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frankfurt_Am_Main-Altstadt-Zerstoerung-Luftbild_1944.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Frankfurt_Am_Main-Altstadt-Zerstoerung-Luftbild_1944.jpg/220px-Frankfurt_Am_Main-Altstadt-Zerstoerung-Luftbild_1944.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Frankfurt_Am_Main-Altstadt-Zerstoerung-Luftbild_1944.jpg/330px-Frankfurt_Am_Main-Altstadt-Zerstoerung-Luftbild_1944.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Frankfurt_Am_Main-Altstadt-Zerstoerung-Luftbild_1944.jpg/440px-Frankfurt_Am_Main-Altstadt-Zerstoerung-Luftbild_1944.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6971" data-file-height="5607" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frankfurt_Am_Main-Altstadt-Zerstoerung-Luftbild_1944.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The area near the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Cathedral" title="Frankfurt Cathedral">Frankfurt Cathedral</a> after a bombing, May 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The purpose of the area bombardment of cities was laid out in a British Air Staff paper, dated 23 September 1941:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The ultimate aim of an attack on a town area is to break the morale of the population which occupies it. To ensure this, we must achieve two things: first, we must make the town physically uninhabitable and, secondly, we must make the people conscious of constant personal danger. The immediate aim, is therefore, twofold, namely, to produce (i) destruction and (ii) fear of death.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>During the first few months of the area bombing campaign, an internal debate within the British government about the most effective use of the nation's limited resources in waging war on Germany continued. Should the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF) be scaled back to allow more resources to go to the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> or should the strategic bombing option be followed and expanded? An influential paper was presented to support the bombing campaign by Professor <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lindemann,_1st_Viscount_Cherwell" title="Frederick Lindemann, 1st Viscount Cherwell">Frederick Lindemann</a>, the British government's leading scientific adviser, justifying the use of area bombing to "<a href="/wiki/De-housing" class="mw-redirect" title="De-housing">dehouse</a>" the German workforce as the most effective way of reducing their morale and affecting enemy war production.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Edward_Singleton" title="John Edward Singleton">Mr. Justice Singleton</a>, a High Court Judge, was asked by Cabinet to look into the competing points of view. In his report, delivered on 20 May 1942, he concluded:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>If Russia can hold Germany on land I doubt whether Germany will stand 12 or 18 months’ continuous, intensified and increased bombing, affecting, as it must, her war production, her power of resistance, her industries and her will to resist (by which I mean morale).<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the end, thanks in part to the dehousing paper, it was this view which prevailed and Bomber Command would remain an important component of the British war effort up to the end of World War II. A large proportion of the industrial production of the United Kingdom was harnessed to the task of creating a vast fleet of heavy bombers. Until 1944, the effect on German production was remarkably small and raised doubts whether it was wise to divert so much effort—the response being there was nowhere else the effort could have been applied, as readily, to greater effect.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Koeln_1945.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Koeln_1945.jpg/220px-Koeln_1945.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Koeln_1945.jpg/330px-Koeln_1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Koeln_1945.jpg/440px-Koeln_1945.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2919" data-file-height="2092" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Koeln_1945.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cologne in 1945, despite being hit dozens of times by Allied bombs, the <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a> survived the war</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lindemann was liked and trusted by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, who appointed him the British government's leading scientific adviser with a seat in the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_(government)" title="Cabinet (government)">Cabinet</a>. In 1942, Lindemann presented the "<a href="/wiki/Dehousing_paper" class="mw-redirect" title="Dehousing paper">dehousing paper</a>" to the Cabinet showing the effect that intensive bombing of German cities could produce. It was accepted by the Cabinet, and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Travers_Harris" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Travers Harris">Air Marshal Harris</a> was appointed to carry out the task. It became an important part of the <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> waged against Germany. Professor Lindemann's paper put forward the theory of attacking major industrial centres in order to deliberately destroy as many homes and houses as possible. Working-class homes were to be targeted because they had a higher density and fire storms were more likely. This would displace the German workforce and reduce their ability to work. His calculations (which were questioned at the time, in particular by Professor <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Blackett,_Baron_Blackett" class="mw-redirect" title="Patrick Blackett, Baron Blackett">P. M. S. Blackett</a> of the Admiralty <a href="/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a> department, expressly refuting Lindemann's conclusions)<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> showed the RAF's <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">Bomber Command</a> would be able to destroy the majority of German houses located in cities quite quickly. The plan was highly controversial even before it started, but the Cabinet thought that bombing was the only option available to directly attack Germany (as a major invasion of the continent was almost two years away), and the Soviets were demanding that the Western Allies do something to relieve the pressure on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Eastern Front</a>. Few in Britain opposed this policy, but there were three notable opponents in Parliament, Bishop <a href="/wiki/George_Bell_(bishop)" title="George Bell (bishop)">George Bell</a> and the Labour <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">MPs</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Stokes" title="Richard Stokes">Richard Stokes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Salter" title="Alfred Salter">Alfred Salter</a>. No effort to examine the effects of bombing was ever made.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 14 February 1942, the <a href="/wiki/Area_bombing_directive" title="Area bombing directive">Area bombing directive</a> was issued to Bomber Command. Bombing was to be "focused on the morale of the enemy civil population and in particular of the industrial workers." Though it was never explicitly declared, this was the nearest that the British got to a declaration of unrestricted aerial bombing – Directive 22 said "You are accordingly authorised to use your forces without restriction", and then listing a series of primary targets which included <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Essen" title="Essen">Essen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duisburg" title="Duisburg">Duisburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>. Secondary targets included <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Braunschweig_in_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombing of Braunschweig in World War II">Braunschweig</a>, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rostock" title="Rostock">Rostock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bremen_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bremen (city)">Bremen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kiel" title="Kiel">Kiel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanover" title="Hanover">Hanover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Schweinfurt" title="Schweinfurt">Schweinfurt</a>. The directive stated that "operations should now be focused on the morale of the enemy civilian population, and in particular, the industrial workers". Lest there be any confusion, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Charles_Portal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Charles Portal">Sir Charles Portal</a> wrote to <a href="/wiki/Air_Chief_Marshal" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Chief Marshal">Air Chief Marshal</a> <a href="/wiki/Norman_Bottomley" title="Norman Bottomley">Norman Bottomley</a> on 15 February "...I suppose it is clear that the aiming points will be the built-up areas, and not, for instance, the dockyards or aircraft factories". Factories were no longer targets.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first true practical demonstrations were on the night of 28 to 29 March 1942, when 234 aircraft <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_L%C3%BCbeck_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Lübeck in World War II">bombed</a> the ancient <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic</a> port of Lübeck. This target was chosen not because it was a significant military target, but because it was expected to be particularly susceptible – in Harris's words it was "built more like a fire lighter than a city". The ancient timber structures burned well, and the raid destroyed most of the city's centre. A few days later, Rostock suffered the same fate.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lancaster_over_Hamburg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lancaster_over_Hamburg.jpg/220px-Lancaster_over_Hamburg.jpg" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lancaster_over_Hamburg.jpg/330px-Lancaster_over_Hamburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lancaster_over_Hamburg.jpg/440px-Lancaster_over_Hamburg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="742" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lancaster_over_Hamburg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Avro_Lancaster" title="Avro Lancaster">Avro Lancaster</a> over <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Hamburg_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Hamburg in World War II">Hamburg</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At this stage of the air war, the most effective and disruptive examples of area bombing were the "thousand-bomber raids". Bomber Command was able by organization and drafting in as many aircraft as possible to assemble very large forces which could then attack a single area, overwhelming the defences. The aircraft would be staggered so that they would arrive over the target in succession: the new technique of the "<a href="/wiki/Bomber_stream" title="Bomber stream">bomber stream</a>".</p>
<p>On 30 May 1942, between 0047 and 0225 hours, in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Millennium" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Millennium">Operation Millennium</a> 1,046 bombers dropped over 2,000 tons of high explosive and incendiaries on the medieval town of <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, and the resulting fires burned it from end to end. The damage inflicted was extensive. The fires could be seen 600 miles away at an altitude of 20,000 feet. Some 3,300 houses were destroyed, and 10,000 were damaged. 12,000 separate fires raged destroying 36 factories, damaging 270 more, and leaving 45,000 people with nowhere to live or to work. Only 384 civilians and 85 soldiers were killed, but thousands evacuated the city. Bomber Command lost 40 bombers.</p>
<p>Two further thousand-bomber raids were conducted over <a href="/wiki/Essen" title="Essen">Essen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bremen_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bremen (city)">Bremen</a>, but neither so utterly shook both sides as the scale of the destruction at Cologne and Hamburg.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The effects of the massive raids using a combination of <a href="/wiki/Blockbuster_bomb" title="Blockbuster bomb">blockbuster bombs</a> (to blow off roofs) and incendiaries (to start fires in the exposed buildings) created <a href="/wiki/Firestorm" title="Firestorm">firestorms</a> in some cities. The most extreme examples of which were caused by <a href="/wiki/Operation_Gomorrah" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Gomorrah">Operation Gomorrah</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Hamburg_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Hamburg in World War II">attack on Hamburg</a>, (45,000 dead), <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Kassel_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Kassel in World War II">attack on Kassel</a> (10,000 dead), the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Darmstadt_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Darmstadt in World War II">attack on Darmstadt</a> (12,500 dead), the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Pforzheim_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Pforzheim in World War II">attack on Pforzheim</a> (21,200 dead), the attack on Swinemuende (23,000 dead) and the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Dresden_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Dresden in World War II">attack on Dresden</a> (25,000 dead).</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1979-025-19A,_Koeln,_Kinderleichen_nach_Luftangriff.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1979-025-19A%2C_Koeln%2C_Kinderleichen_nach_Luftangriff.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1979-025-19A%2C_Koeln%2C_Kinderleichen_nach_Luftangriff.jpg" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1979-025-19A%2C_Koeln%2C_Kinderleichen_nach_Luftangriff.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1979-025-19A%2C_Koeln%2C_Kinderleichen_nach_Luftangriff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1979-025-19A%2C_Koeln%2C_Kinderleichen_nach_Luftangriff.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1979-025-19A%2C_Koeln%2C_Kinderleichen_nach_Luftangriff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="573" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1979-025-19A,_Koeln,_Kinderleichen_nach_Luftangriff.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An elderly woman in front of the bodies of school children in <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, Germany, after a bombing raid</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to economic historian <a href="/wiki/Adam_Tooze" title="Adam Tooze">Adam Tooze</a>, in his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wages_of_Destruction" title="The Wages of Destruction">The Wages of Destruction: The Making and Breaking of the Nazi Economy</a></i>, a turning point in the bomber offensive was reached in March 1943, during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Ruhr" title="Battle of the Ruhr">Battle of the Ruhr</a>. Over five months 34,000 tons of bombs were dropped. Following the raids, steel production fell by 200,000 tons, making a shortfall of 400,000 tons. Speer acknowledged that the RAF were hitting the right targets, and raids severely disrupted his plans to increase production to meet increasing attritional needs. Between July 1943 and March 1944 there were no further increases in the output of aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>The bombing of Hamburg in 1943 also produced impressive results. <a href="/wiki/Tiger_I" title="Tiger I">Tiger tank</a> production, and the manufacture of 88mm guns, the most potent dual-purpose artillery piece in the <i>Wehrmacht</i> was "set back for months". On top of this, some 62 percent of the population was dehoused causing more difficulties. However, RAF Bomber Command allowed itself to be distracted by Harris' desire for a war winning blow, and attempted the fruitless missions to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Berlin_(air)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Berlin (air)">destroy Berlin</a> and end the war by spring, 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 1943, Harris urged the government to be honest with the public regarding the purpose of the bombing campaign. To Harris, his complete success at Hamburg confirmed the validity and necessity of his methods, and he urged that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>the aim of the Combined Bomber Offensive...should be unambiguously stated [as] the destruction of German cities, the killing of German workers, and the disruption of civilized life throughout Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p><br />
... the destruction of houses, public utilities, transport and lives, the creation of a refugee problem on an unprecedented scale, and the breakdown of morale both at home and at the battle fronts by fear of extended and intensified bombing, are accepted and intended aims of our bombing policy. They are not by-products of attempts to hit factories.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>By contrast, the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing_survey" class="mw-redirect" title="Strategic bombing survey">United States Strategic Bombing Survey</a> found attacks on waterways, beginning 23 September with strikes against the <a href="/wiki/Dortmund-Ems_Canal" class="mw-redirect" title="Dortmund-Ems Canal">Dortmund-Ems Canal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mittelland_Canal" title="Mittelland Canal">Mittelland Canal</a>, produced tremendous traffic problems on the Rhine River. It had immediate impacts on shipments of goods, and especially coal deliveries, upon which Germany's economy depended; with no more additional effort, by February 1945, rail transport (which competed for coal) had seen its shipments cut by more than half, and by March, "except in limited areas, the coal supply had been eliminated."<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>The devastating bombing raids of <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a> at the 12th March 1945 with 1,108 aircraft – 748 Lancasters, 292 Halifaxes, 68 Mosquitos – was a record attack on a single target in the whole of World War II. More than 4,800 tonnage of bombs was dropped through the city centre and the south of the city and destroyed 98% of buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_British_efforts">Other British efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other British efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Operation_Chastise" title="Operation Chastise">Operation Chastise</a>, better known as the Dambusters raid, was an attempt to damage German industrial production by crippling its hydro-electric power and transport in the Ruhr area. <a href="/wiki/Operation_Hydra_(1943)" title="Operation Hydra (1943)">Operation Hydra</a> of August 1943 sought to destroy German work on long-range rockets but only delayed it by a few months. Subsequent efforts were directed against <a href="/wiki/V-weapon" class="mw-redirect" title="V-weapon">V-weapon</a> launch sites in France.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_bombing_in_Europe">US bombing in Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: US bombing in Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg/220px-Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg/330px-Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg/440px-Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fotothek_df_ps_0000010_Blick_vom_Rathausturm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>, 1945, view from the city hall (Rathaus) over the destroyed city</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ussb-1.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/Ussb-1.svg/400px-Ussb-1.svg.png" width="400" height="645" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/Ussb-1.svg/600px-Ussb-1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/Ussb-1.svg/800px-Ussb-1.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="591" data-file-height="953" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ussb-1.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Summary of AAF and RAF bombing .<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In mid 1942, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">United States Army Air Forces</a> (USAAF) arrived in the UK and carried out a few raids across the English Channel. The USAAF <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Air_Force" title="Eighth Air Force">Eighth Air Force</a>'s B-17 bombers were called the "Flying Fortresses" because of their heavy defensive armament of ten to twelve machine guns — eventually comprising up to thirteen heavy 12.7&#160;mm calibre, <a href="/wiki/Browning_M2#AN.2FM2" class="mw-redirect" title="Browning M2">"light barrel" Browning M2</a> guns per bomber — and armor plating in vital locations. In part because of their heavier armament and armor, they carried smaller bomb loads than British bombers. With all of this, the USAAF's commanders in Washington, D.C., and in Great Britain adopted the strategy of taking on the Luftwaffe head on, in larger and larger air raids by mutually defending bombers, flying over Germany, Austria, and France at high altitudes during the daytime. Also, both the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Government" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Government">U.S. Government</a> and its Army Air Forces commanders were reluctant to bomb enemy cities and towns indiscriminately. They claimed that by using the B-17 and the <a href="/wiki/Norden_bombsight" title="Norden bombsight">Norden bombsight</a>, the USAAF should be able to carry out "<a href="/wiki/Precision_bombing" title="Precision bombing">precision bombing</a>" on locations vital to the German war machine: factories, naval bases, shipyards, railroad yards, railroad junctions, power plants, steel mills, <a href="/wiki/Airfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Airfield">airfields</a>, etc.</p>
<p>In January 1943, at the <a href="/wiki/Casablanca_Conference" title="Casablanca Conference">Casablanca Conference</a>, it was agreed RAF Bomber Command operations against Germany would be reinforced by the USAAF in a Combined Operations Offensive plan called <a href="/wiki/Operation_Pointblank" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Pointblank">Operation Pointblank</a>. Chief of the British Air Staff <a href="/wiki/Marshal_of_the_Royal_Air_Force" title="Marshal of the Royal Air Force">MRAF</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Portal,_1st_Viscount_Portal_of_Hungerford" title="Charles Portal, 1st Viscount Portal of Hungerford">Sir Charles Portal</a> was put in charge of the "strategic direction" of both British and American bomber operations. The text of the <a href="/wiki/Casablanca_directive" title="Casablanca directive">Casablanca directive</a> read: "Your primary object will be the progressive destruction and dislocation of the German military, industrial, and economic system and the undermining of the morale of the German people to a point where their capacity for armed resistance is fatally weakened."<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> At the beginning of the combined strategic bombing offensive on 4 March 1943, 669 RAF and 303 USAAF heavy bombers were available.</p>
<p>In late 1943, the 'Pointblank' attacks manifested themselves in the infamous Schweinfurt raids (<a href="/wiki/Schweinfurt%E2%80%93Regensburg_mission" title="Schweinfurt–Regensburg mission">first</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Raid_on_Schweinfurt" title="Second Raid on Schweinfurt">second</a>). Formations of unescorted bombers were no match for German fighters, which inflicted a deadly toll. In despair, the Eighth halted air operations over Germany until a long-range fighter could be found in 1944; it proved to be the <a href="/wiki/P-51_Mustang" class="mw-redirect" title="P-51 Mustang">P-51 Mustang</a>, which had the range to fly to Berlin and back.</p>
<p>USAAF leaders firmly held to the claim of "precision bombing" of military targets for much of the war, and dismissed claims they were simply bombing cities. However the American Eighth Air Force received the first <a href="/wiki/H2X_radar" class="mw-redirect" title="H2X radar">H2X radar</a> sets in December 1943. Within two weeks of the arrival of these first six sets, the Eighth command gave permission for them to area bomb a city using H2X and would continue to authorize, on average, about one such attack a week until the end of the war in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>In reality, the day bombing was "precision bombing" only in the sense that most bombs fell somewhere near a specific designated target such as a railway yard. Conventionally, the air forces designated as "the target area" a circle having a radius of 1,000 feet (300m) around the aiming point of attack. While accuracy improved during the war, Survey studies show that, overall, only about 20% of the bombs aimed at precision targets fell within this target area.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> In the fall of 1944, only seven percent of all bombs dropped by the Eighth Air Force hit within 1,000 feet of their aim point.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, the sheer tonnage of explosive delivered by day and by night was eventually sufficient to cause widespread damage, and, more importantly from a military point of view, forced Germany to divert resources to counter it. This was to be the real significance of the Allied strategic bombing campaign—resource allocation.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-08778-0001,_Dresden,_Tote_nach_Bombenangriff.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-08778-0001%2C_Dresden%2C_Tote_nach_Bombenangriff.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-08778-0001%2C_Dresden%2C_Tote_nach_Bombenangriff.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-08778-0001%2C_Dresden%2C_Tote_nach_Bombenangriff.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-08778-0001%2C_Dresden%2C_Tote_nach_Bombenangriff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-08778-0001%2C_Dresden%2C_Tote_nach_Bombenangriff.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-08778-0001%2C_Dresden%2C_Tote_nach_Bombenangriff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="575" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-08778-0001,_Dresden,_Tote_nach_Bombenangriff.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A pile of bodies in Dresden before cremation</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For the sake of improving USAAF <a href="/wiki/Firebombing" title="Firebombing">firebombing</a> capabilities, a mock-up <a href="/wiki/German_Village_(Dugway_proving_ground)" class="mw-redirect" title="German Village (Dugway proving ground)">German Village</a> was built up and repeatedly burned down. It contained full-scale replicas of German residential homes. Firebombing attacks proved quite successful, in a series of attacks in July 1943 on Hamburg, roughly 50,000 civilians were killed and large areas of the city destroyed.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:8th_AF_Bombing_Marienburg.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/8th_AF_Bombing_Marienburg.JPEG/220px-8th_AF_Bombing_Marienburg.JPEG" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/8th_AF_Bombing_Marienburg.JPEG/330px-8th_AF_Bombing_Marienburg.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/8th_AF_Bombing_Marienburg.JPEG/440px-8th_AF_Bombing_Marienburg.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="2716" data-file-height="2187" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:8th_AF_Bombing_Marienburg.JPEG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A raid by the <a href="/wiki/8th_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="8th Air Force">8th Air Force</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Focke_Wulf" class="mw-redirect" title="Focke Wulf">Focke Wulf</a> factory at <a href="/wiki/Malbork" title="Malbork">Marienburg</a>, Germany (1943).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the arrival of the brand-new <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Fifteenth Air Force">Fifteenth Air Force</a> based in Italy, command of the U.S. Air Forces in Europe was consolidated into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Strategic_Air_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Strategic Air Forces">United States Strategic Air Forces</a> (USSTAF). With the addition of the Mustang to its strength — and a <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Doolittle#Doolittle.27s_breakthrough_in_fighter_tactics" title="Jimmy Doolittle">major change</a> in fighter tactics by the Eighth Air Force, meant to secure daylight <a href="/wiki/Air_supremacy" title="Air supremacy">air supremacy</a> for the Americans over Germany from the start of 1944 onwards — the <a href="/wiki/Combined_Bomber_Offensive" title="Combined Bomber Offensive">Combined Bomber Offensive</a> was resumed. Planners targeted the <i>Luftwaffe</i> in an operation known as '<a href="/wiki/Big_Week" title="Big Week">Big Week</a>' (20–25 February 1944) and succeeded brilliantly – its major attacks came during the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Steinbock" title="Operation Steinbock">"Baby Blitz"</a> period for the Luftwaffe over England, while losses for the Luftwaffe's day fighter forces were so heavy that both the twin-engined <i>Zerstörergeschwader</i> <a href="/wiki/Heavy_fighter" title="Heavy fighter">heavy fighter</a> wings (the intended main anti-bomber force) and their replacement, single-engined <i>Sturmgruppen</i> of <a href="/wiki/Focke-Wulf_Fw_190_operational_history#The_Sturmb.C3.B6cke" title="Focke-Wulf Fw 190 operational history">heavily armed Fw 190As</a> became largely ineffective, clearing each force of <a href="/wiki/Bomber_destroyer" title="Bomber destroyer">bomber destroyers</a> in their turn from Germany's skies throughout most of 1944. With such heavy losses of their primary means of defense against the USAAF's tactics, German planners were forced into a hasty dispersal of industry, with the day fighter arm never being able to fully recover in time.</p>
<p>On 27 March 1944, the <a href="/wiki/Combined_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Combined Chiefs of Staff">Combined Chiefs of Staff</a> issued orders granting control of all the Allied air forces in Europe, including strategic bombers, to General <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, the Supreme Allied Commander, who delegated command to his deputy in <a href="/wiki/SHAEF" class="mw-redirect" title="SHAEF">SHAEF</a> Air Chief Marshal <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Tedder,_1st_Baron_Tedder" title="Arthur Tedder, 1st Baron Tedder">Arthur Tedder</a>. There was resistance to this order from some senior figures, including <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, Harris, and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Spaatz" class="mw-redirect" title="Carl Spaatz">Carl Spaatz</a>, but after some debate, control passed to SHAEF on 1 April 1944. When the Combined Bomber Offensive officially ended on 1 April, Allied airmen were well on the way to achieving air superiority over all of Europe. While they continued some strategic bombing, the USAAF along with the RAF turned their attention to the tactical air battle in support of the <a href="/wiki/Normandy_Invasion" class="mw-redirect" title="Normandy Invasion">Normandy Invasion</a>. It was not until the middle of September that the strategic bombing campaign of Germany again became the priority for the <a href="/wiki/USSTAF" class="mw-redirect" title="USSTAF">USSTAF</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NL309-312_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NL309-312-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>The twin campaigns—the USAAF by day, the RAF by night—built up into massive bombing of German industrial areas, notably <a href="/wiki/Ruhr_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruhr area">the Ruhr</a>, followed by attacks directly on cities such as <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Hamburg_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Hamburg in World War II">Hamburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Kassel_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Kassel in World War II">Kassel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Pforzheim_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Pforzheim in World War II">Pforzheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Mainz_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Mainz in World War II">Mainz</a> and the often-criticized <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Dresden_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Dresden in World War II">bombing of Dresden</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bombing_in_Italy">Bombing in Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Bombing in Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Italy, first as an Axis member and later as a German-occupied country, was heavily bombed by Allied forces for all the duration of the war. In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">Northern Italy</a>, after small-scale bombings which mainly targeted factories, only causing little damage and casualties, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">RAF Bomber Command</a> launched a first large-scale <a href="/wiki/Area_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Area bombing">area bombing</a> campaign on <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a> (the so-called ‘industrial triangle’) during the autumn of 1942. All three cities suffered heavy damage and hundreds on civilian casualties, although the effects were less disastrous than those suffered by German cities, mainly because Italian cities had centers made of <a href="/wiki/Brick" title="Brick">brick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone">stone</a> buildings, while German cities had centers made of wooden buildings. Milan and Turin were bombed again in February 1943; the heaviest raids were carried out in July (295 bombers dropped 763 tons of bombs on Turin, killing 792 people) and August (all three cities were bombed and a total of 843 bombers dropped 2,268 tons of bombs over Milan, causing about 900 casualties). These attacks caused widespread damage and prompted most of the cities’ inhabitants to flee. During 1944 and 1945 Milan, Turin and Genoa were instead bombed by <a href="/wiki/USAAF" class="mw-redirect" title="USAAF">USAAF</a> bombers, which mainly targeted factories and <a href="/wiki/Marshalling_yard" class="mw-redirect" title="Marshalling yard">marshalling yards</a>; nonetheless, imprecision in bombings caused further destruction of vast areas. By the end of the war, about 30–40% of the buildings in each of the three cities were destroyed, and both in Milan and Turin less than half of the city remained undamaged.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> 2,069 people were killed in Turin<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Milan_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Milan in World War II">over 2,200 in Milan</a>. The only other city in Italy to be subjected to area bombing was <a href="/wiki/La_Spezia" title="La Spezia">La Spezia</a>, heavily bombed by the Bomber Command during April 1943, with slight casualties but massive damage (45% of the buildings were destroyed or heavily damaged,<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> and just 25–30% remained undamaged). Several other cities in Northern Italy suffered heay damage and casualties due to USAAF bombings, usually aimed at factories and marshalling yards but often inaccurate; among them <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> (2,481 casualties<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a> (about 2,000 casualties<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Rimini" title="Rimini">Rimini</a> (98% of the city was destroyed or damaged<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Treviso" title="Treviso">Treviso</a> (1,600 killed in the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Treviso_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Treviso in World War II">bombing of 7 April 1944</a>, 80% of the city destroyed or damaged<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a> (463 casualties on 10 June 1944<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Vicenza" title="Vicenza">Vicenza</a> (317 casualties on 18 November 1944).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Milan_after_Bombing.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Milan_after_Bombing.jpg/220px-Milan_after_Bombing.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Milan_after_Bombing.jpg/330px-Milan_after_Bombing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Milan_after_Bombing.jpg/440px-Milan_after_Bombing.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3640" data-file-height="2456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Milan_after_Bombing.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Building destroyed in Milan after the August 1943 bombings. <a href="/wiki/Milan_Cathedral" title="Milan Cathedral">Milan Cathedral</a> is visible in the background.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a>, after small-scale bombings by the RAF (more frequent than in the North), USAAF started its bombing campaign in December 1942. The bombings mostly targeted <a href="/wiki/Harbour" class="mw-redirect" title="Harbour">harbour</a> facilities, marshalling yards, factories and <a href="/wiki/Airport" title="Airport">airports</a>, but the inaccuracy of the attacks caused extensive destruction and civilian casualties; among the cities hit the hardest were <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> (6,000 casualties<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Messina" title="Messina">Messina</a> (more than one third of the city was destroyed,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> and only 30% remained untouched), <a href="/wiki/Reggio_Calabria" title="Reggio Calabria">Reggio Calabria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foggia" title="Foggia">Foggia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Foggia" title="Bombing of Foggia">thousands of casualties</a>), <a href="/wiki/Cagliari" title="Cagliari">Cagliari</a> (416 inhabitants were killed in the bombings of February 1943, 80% of the city was damaged or destroyed), <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catania" title="Catania">Catania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trapani" title="Trapani">Trapani</a> (70% of the buildings were damaged or destroyed<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Central_Italy" title="Central Italy">Central Italy</a> was left untouched for the first three years of war, but from 1943 onwards it was heavily bombed by USAAF, with heavy damage (usually due to inaccuracy in bombing) to a number of cities, including <a href="/wiki/Livorno" title="Livorno">Livorno</a> (57% of the city was destroyed or damaged, over 500 people were killed in June 1943), <a href="/wiki/Civitavecchia" title="Civitavecchia">Civitavecchia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grosseto" title="Grosseto">Grosseto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terni" title="Terni">Terni</a> (1,077 casualties<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a> (1,738 casualties<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Pescara" title="Pescara">Pescara</a> (between 2,200 and 3,900 casualties), <a href="/wiki/Ancona" title="Ancona">Ancona</a> (1,182 casualties<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Viterbo" title="Viterbo">Viterbo</a> (1,017 casualties<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Isernia" title="Isernia">Isernia</a> (about 500 casualties on 11 September 1943). <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Rome" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombing of Rome">was bombed on several occasions</a>; the historic centre and the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican</a> were spared, but the <a href="/wiki/Suburb" title="Suburb">suburbs</a> suffered heavy damage and between 3,000 and 5,000 casualties. <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> also suffered some bombings in the outskirts (215 people were killed on 25 September 1943), while the historical centre was not bombed. <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> proper was never bombed.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatia</a>, the Italian <a href="/wiki/Enclave" class="mw-redirect" title="Enclave">enclave</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zadar" title="Zadar">Zara</a> <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Zara_in_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombing of Zara in World War II">suffered extensive bombing</a>, which destroyed 60% of the city and killed about 1,000 of its 20,000 inhabitants, prompting most of the population to flee to mainland Italy (the town was later annexed to <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>).</p>
<p>Except for Rome, Venice, Florence, <a href="/wiki/Urbino" title="Urbino">Urbino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a>, damage to <a href="/wiki/Cultural_heritage" title="Cultural heritage">cultural heritage</a> in Italy was widespread.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bombing_in_France">Bombing in France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Bombing in France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>German-occupied France contained a number of important targets that attracted the attention of the British, and later American bombing. In 1940, RAF Bomber Command launched attacks against German preparations for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sealion" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Sealion">Operation Sealion</a>, the proposed invasion of England, attacking Channel Ports in France and Belgium and sinking large numbers of barges that had been collected by the Germans for use in the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-Hardest_p84-7_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hardest_p84-7-186">[186]</a></sup> France's Atlantic ports were important bases for both German surface ships and submarines, while French industry was an important contributor to the German war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-exeter_exhibition_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exeter_exhibition-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before 1944, the Allies bombed targets in France that were part of the German war industry. This included raids such as those on the <a href="/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault</a> factory in <a href="/wiki/Boulogne-Billancourt" title="Boulogne-Billancourt">Boulogne-Billancourt</a> in March 1942 or the port facilities of <a href="/wiki/Nantes" title="Nantes">Nantes</a> in September 1943 (which killed 1,500 civilians). In preparation of Allied <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Normandy" title="Invasion of Normandy">landings in Normandy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Dragoon" title="Operation Dragoon">those in the south of France</a>, French infrastructure (mainly rail transport) was intensively targeted by RAF and USAAF in May and June 1944. Despite intelligence provided by the <a href="/wiki/French_Resistance" title="French Resistance">French Resistance</a>, many residential areas were hit in error or lack of accuracy. This included cities like <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a> (2,000 dead), <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a> (1,000 dead), <a href="/wiki/Saint-%C3%89tienne" title="Saint-Étienne">Saint-Étienne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Orléans">Orléans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grenoble" title="Grenoble">Grenoble</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nice" title="Nice">Nice</a>, Paris surrounds, and so on. The <a href="/wiki/Free_French_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French Air Force">Free French Air Force</a>, operational since 1941, used to opt for the more risky skimming tactic when operating in national territory, to avoid civilian casualties. On 5 January 1945, British bombers struck the "Atlantic pocket" of <a href="/wiki/Royan" title="Royan">Royan</a> and destroyed 85% of this city. A later raid, using <a href="/wiki/Napalm" title="Napalm">napalm</a> was carried out before it was freed from Nazi occupation in April. Of the 3,000 civilians left in the city, 442 died.</p>
<p>French civilian casualties due to Allied strategic bombing are estimated at about half of the 67,000 French civilian dead during Allied operations in 1942–1945; the other part being mostly killed during tactical bombing in the Normandy campaign. 22% of the bombs dropped in Europe by British and American air forces between 1940 and 1945 were in France.<sup id="cite_ref-Florentin_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Florentin-188">[188]</a></sup> The port city of <a href="/wiki/Le_Havre" title="Le Havre">Le Havre</a> had been destroyed by 132 bombings during the war (5,000 dead) until September 1944. It has been rebuilt by architect <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Perret" title="Auguste Perret">Auguste Perret</a> and is now a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_strategic_bombing">Soviet strategic bombing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Soviet strategic bombing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_bombing" title="Shuttle bombing">Shuttle bombing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Frantic" title="Operation Frantic">Operation Frantic</a></div>
<p>The first Soviet offensive bomber campaign was directed against the <a href="/wiki/Ploie%C8%99ti" title="Ploiești">Romanian oilfields</a> in the summer of 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> In response to a German raid on Moscow on the night of 21–22 July 1941, <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Naval_Aviation" title="Soviet Naval Aviation">Soviet Naval Aviation</a> launched a series of seven raids against Germany, primarily Berlin, between the night of 7–8 August and 3–4 September. These attacks were undertaken by between four and fifteen aircraft—beginning on 11 August the new <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_TB-7" class="mw-redirect" title="Tupolev TB-7">Tupolev TB-7</a>—from the island of <a href="/wiki/Saaremaa" title="Saaremaa">Saaremaa</a>, base of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=1st_Torpedo_Air_Regiment&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="1st Torpedo Air Regiment (page does not exist)">1st Torpedo Air Regiment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> (At least one raid of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=81st_Air_Division&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="81st Air Division (page does not exist)">81st Air Division</a> took off from <a href="/wiki/Pushkin,_Russia" title="Pushkin, Russia">Pushkin</a>.) Besides thirty tonnes of bombs, they also dropped leaflets with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>'s defiant speech of 3 July. The Soviets sent a total of 549 long-range bombers over German territory in all of 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1942 the strategic bombing arm of the Soviet Union was reorganized as the <a href="/wiki/Long_Range_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Long Range Air Force">Long Range Air Force</a> (ADD). It raided Berlin from 26–29 August and again on the night of 9–10 September with 212 planes.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> It raided <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a> for the first time on 24 August, <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a> on 4–5 and 9–10 September and <a href="/wiki/Bucharest" title="Bucharest">Bucharest</a> on 13–14 September. The German-occupied Polish cities of <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> were not exempt, but the bombers concentrated primarily on military targets.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> There were 1,114 sorties over Germany in 1942. In March 1943 there was a strategic shift: in preparation for the <a href="/wiki/Kursk_Offensive" class="mw-redirect" title="Kursk Offensive">Kursk Offensive</a>, the bombers were directed against the German railroads behind the front.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> In April the Long Range Air Force expanded to eight air corps and eleven independent divisions containing 700 planes. After the Kursk preparations, the Soviets turned their attention to administrative and industrial targets in <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a> in April. With 920 aircraft taking part, they dropped 700 tonnes of bombs there. The largest Soviet bomb of the war, an 11,000-pound weapon, was dropped on <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsberg" title="Königsberg">Königsberg</a> during one of these raids.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout 1943, the Soviets attempted to give the impression of cooperation between their bombers and those of the West.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> In February 1944 they again shifted priority, this time towards <a href="/wiki/Terror_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Terror bombing">terror bombing</a>, with the goal of knocking Finland and Hungary out of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> Helsinki was struck by 733 bombers on the night of 6–7 February, by 367 on the 15–16th and 850 on the 25–26th. A total of 2,386 tonnes of bombs were dropped.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> Budapest was hit four straight nights from 13–20 September with a total of 8,000 tonnes by 1,129 bombers. The Soviets flew 4,466 sorties into enemy territory in the year 1944. In December the Long Range Air Force was reorganized as the <a href="/w/index.php?title=18th_Air_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="18th Air Army (page does not exist)">18th Air Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main task of the 18th Air Army was to support the final offensive against Germany, but it also undertook raids against Berlin, <a href="/wiki/Breslau" class="mw-redirect" title="Breslau">Breslau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danzig" class="mw-redirect" title="Danzig">Danzig</a> and Königsberg.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> In total, 7,158 Soviet aircraft dropped 6,700 tonnes of bombs on Germany during the war, a modest 3.1% of Soviet bomber sorties, a mere 0.5% of all Allied "strategic" sorties against German-occupied territory and a measly 0.2% of all bombs dropped on it.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the war, Marxist historians in the Soviet Union and East Germany claimed that the Soviet strategic bombing campaign was limited by moral qualms over bombing civilian centres.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> One early bombing theorist, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vasili_Chripin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vasili Chripin (page does not exist)">Vasili Chripin</a>, whose theories influenced the Soviet Union's first strategic bombing guidelines (1936) and the service regulations of 26 January 1940, drew back from terror bombing as advocated by Western theorists.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> also convinced Soviet war planners that the air force was most effective when used in close cooperation with ground forces. Nonetheless, after the war, Marshal <a href="/wiki/Vasili_Sokolovsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Vasili Sokolovsky">Vasili Sokolovsky</a> admitted that the Soviets would have gladly launched a strategic bombing offensive had they the capability.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup> In reality, the Soviets never geared aircraft production towards long-range bombers, beyond the small force of indigenously designed and produced <a href="/wiki/Petlyakov_Pe-8" title="Petlyakov Pe-8">Petlyakov Pe-8</a> four-engined "heavies", and so never had enough to mount an effective campaign. The land-based nature of warfare on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Eastern Front</a> also required closer cooperation between the air forces and ground troops than did, for example, the defence of Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Soviet_189-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effectiveness">Effectiveness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Effectiveness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Strategic bombing has been criticized on practical grounds because it does not work predictably. The radical changes it forces on a targeted population can backfire, including the counterproductive result of freeing inessential labourers to fill worker shortages in war industries.<sup id="cite_ref-J.K._Galbraith_1958_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J.K._Galbraith_1958-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Much of the doubt about the effectiveness of the bomber war comes from the fact German industrial production increased throughout the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Williamson_Murray_p._319_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williamson_Murray_p._319-24">[24]</a></sup> A combination of factors helped increase German war material output, these included; continuing development from production lines started before the war, limiting competing models of equipment, government enforced sharing of production techniques, a change in how contracts were priced and an aggressive worker suggestion program. At the same time production plants had to deal with a loss of experienced workers to the military, assimilating untrained workers, culling workers incapable of being trained, and utilizing unwilling forced labor. Strategic bombing failed to reduce German war production. There is insufficient information to ascertain how much additional potential industrial growth the bombing campaign may have curtailed.<sup id="cite_ref-econ.yale.edu_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econ.yale.edu-25">[25]</a></sup> However, attacks on the infrastructure were taking place. The attacks on Germany's canals and railroads made transportation of <a href="/wiki/Materiel" title="Materiel">materiel</a> difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-anesi.com_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anesi.com-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Oil_Campaign_of_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil Campaign of World War II">Oil Campaign of World War II</a> was, however, extremely successful and made a very large contribution to the general collapse of Germany in 1945. In the event, the bombing of oil facilities became <a href="/wiki/Albert_Speer" title="Albert Speer">Albert Speer</a>'s main concern; however, this occurred sufficiently late in the war that Germany would soon be defeated in any case.</p>
<p>German insiders credit the Allied bombing offensive with crippling the German war industry. Speer repeatedly said (both during and after the war) it caused crucial production problems. Admiral <a href="/wiki/Karl_D%C3%B6nitz" title="Karl Dönitz">Karl Dönitz</a>, head of the <a href="/wiki/U-boat" title="U-boat">U-boat</a> fleet (<i>U-waffe</i>), noted in his memoirs failure to get the revolutionary <a href="/wiki/German_Type_XXI_submarine" class="mw-redirect" title="German Type XXI submarine">Type XXI</a> U-boats (which could have completely altered the balance of power in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">Battle of the Atlantic</a>) into service was entirely the result of the bombing. The <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Bombing_Survey_(Europe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Strategic Bombing Survey (Europe)">United States Strategic Bombing Survey (Europe)</a>, says, despite bombing becoming a major effort, between December 1942 and June 1943, "The attack on the construction yards and slipways was not heavy enough to be more than troublesome" and the delays in delivery of Type XXIs and <a href="/wiki/German_Type_XXIII_submarine" class="mw-redirect" title="German Type XXIII submarine">XXIIIs</a> up until November 1944 "cannot be attributed to the air attack",<sup id="cite_ref-anesi.com_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anesi.com-22">[22]</a></sup> but adds, "The attacks during the late winter and early spring of 1945 did close, or all but close, five of the major yards, including the great Blohm and Voss plant at Hamburg".<sup id="cite_ref-anesi.com_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anesi.com-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_morale">Effect on morale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Effect on morale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Effects_of_strategic_bombing_on_German_morale.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/Effects_of_strategic_bombing_on_German_morale.jpg/220px-Effects_of_strategic_bombing_on_German_morale.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/Effects_of_strategic_bombing_on_German_morale.jpg/330px-Effects_of_strategic_bombing_on_German_morale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/15/Effects_of_strategic_bombing_on_German_morale.jpg 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="584" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Effects_of_strategic_bombing_on_German_morale.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram showing the effects of strategic bombing on German morale in 1945. From the United States <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Bombing_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="Strategic Bombing Survey">Strategic Bombing Survey</a>, Morale Division.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although designed to "break the enemy's will", the opposite often happened. The British did not crumble under the German <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">Blitz</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">air raids</a> early in the war. British workers continued to work throughout the war and food and other basic supplies were available throughout.</p>
<p>The impact of bombing on German morale was significant according to Professor <a href="/wiki/John_Buckley_(historian)" title="John Buckley (historian)">John Buckley</a>. Around a third of the urban population under threat of bombing had no protection at all. Some of the major cities saw 55–60 percent of dwellings destroyed. Mass evacuations were a partial answer for six million civilians, but this had a severe impact on morale as German families were split up to live in difficult conditions. By 1944 absenteeism rates of 20–25 percent were not unusual and in post-war analysis 91 percent of civilians stated bombing was the most difficult hardship to endure and was the key factor in the collapse of their own morale.<sup id="cite_ref-Buckley_1998.2C_p._166_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley_1998.2C_p._166-190">[190]</a></sup> The United States Strategic Bombing Survey concluded that the bombing was not stiffening morale but seriously depressing it; fatalism, apathy, defeatism were apparent in bombed areas. The <i>Luftwaffe</i> was blamed for not warding off the attacks and confidence in the Nazi regime fell by 14 percent. By the spring of 1944, some 75 percent of Germans believed the war was lost, owing to the intensity of the bombing.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buckley argues the German war economy did indeed expand significantly following <a href="/wiki/Albert_Speer" title="Albert Speer">Albert Speer</a>'s appointment as Reichsminister of Armaments, "but it is spurious to argue that because production increased then bombing had no real impact". The bombing offensive did do serious damage to German production levels. German tank and aircraft production, though reached new records in production levels in 1944, was in particular one-third lower than planned.<sup id="cite_ref-Buckley_1998.2C_p._165_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley_1998.2C_p._165-26">[26]</a></sup> In fact, German aircraft production for 1945 was planned at 80,000, showing Erhard Milch and other leading German planners were pushing for even higher outputs; "unhindered by Allied bombing German production would have risen far higher".<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983.2C_p._253_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983.2C_p._253-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Journalist <a href="/wiki/Max_Hastings" title="Max Hastings">Max Hastings</a> and the authors of the official history of the bomber offensive, <a href="/wiki/Noble_Frankland" title="Noble Frankland">Noble Frankland</a> among them, has argued bombing had a limited effect on morale. In the words of the British Bombing Survey Unit (BBSU), "The essential premise behind the policy of treating towns as unit targets for area attack, namely that the German economic system was fully extended, was false." This, the BBSU noted, was because official estimates of German war production were "more than 100 percent in excess of the true figures". The BBSU concluded, "Far from there being any evidence of a cumulative effect on (German) war production, it is evident that, as the (bombing) offensive progressed ... the effect on war production became progressively smaller (and) did not reach significant dimensions."<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allied_bombing_statistics_1939.E2.80.9345">Allied bombing statistics 1939–45</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Allied bombing statistics 1939–45">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the United States Strategic Bombing Survey, Allied bombers between 1939 and 1945 dropped 1,415,745 tons of bombs over Germany (51.1% of the total bomb tonnage dropped by Allied bombers in the European campaign), 570,730 tons over France (20.6%), 379,565 tons over Italy (13.7%), 185,625 tons over Austria, Hungary and the Balkans (6.7%), and 218,873 tons over other countries (7.9%).<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p>

<tr>
